[01:10 - 01:17] So we have the same general assumptions as before. No real changes from how you would model any old DCF.
[01:18 - 01:25] Then the free cash will build up is identical to a standard DCF exercise. You're looking at unlevered free cash flows.
[01:25 - 01:32] So you're referencing in EBITDA, EBIT, ultimately to get to earnings before interest after taxes.
[01:32 - 01:38] Again, these, this color font represents that you are referencing in from another worksheet.
[01:38 - 01:42] And so this is coming straight from our income statement.
[01:43 - 01:46] You want to make sure that you've got all the row headers synced up.
[01:46 - 01:53] Then you make all the adjustments to interest earnings before interest and after taxes that we've made in our DCF modeling course.
[01:53 - 01:57] You want to basically make all these non cash ad backs like depreciation,
[01:57 - 02:02] amortization, stock based compensation, et cetera until you get to unlevered cash from operations.
[02:02 - 02:09] This unlevered cash from operations. Then you have to take into account all of the outflows capital for capital spending.
[02:10 - 02:15] And so this again comes from our spending on purchases of intangible assets, capital expenditures,
[02:15 - 02:22] as well as a pretty significant line item for BMC, which is the purchases which are capitalized software development costs.
[02:22 - 02:27] So rather than expensing those, they are capitalized in amortized.
[02:27 - 02:33] So with that, we have to calculate a discount factor for those that went through our DCF course recall that
[02:33 - 02:37] when you're building a model in the middle of the period. So this is an annual model.
[02:37 - 02:43] We're trying to figure out the value. What is the fair value for BMC as of May 6, 2013?
[02:43 - 02:48] Well, in that case, you want to make sure that you're applying a major adjustment factor to your discounting.
[02:48 - 02:52] So that you start with a nominal undiscounted unlevered free cash flow here.
[02:52 - 02:59] And then after the adjustments for discounting, you get to the next five years worth of unlevered free cash flows.
[02:59 - 03:02] They're present value are as illustrated.
[03:02 - 03:06] Then you go through the process of looking at really two approaches.
[03:06 - 03:12] One is calculating the terminal value, the value beyond 2018 using the perpetuity approach,
[03:12 - 03:18] which has you explicitly identify a long-term growth rate of these cash flows that you forecast here
[03:18 - 03:25] of this 870 million in cash flows. And we are assigning a 0% long-term growth rate here simply
[03:25 - 03:30] because BMC appears to be growing very slowly already towards the end of its five years.
[03:30 - 03:33] This is a company whose revenues are declining as we've already talked about.
[03:35 - 03:39] And so beyond this phase when we're looking at mature phase, we're going to assume virtually no
[03:39 - 03:45] growth whatsoever to be conservative. And that gets us to a total enterprise value when after counting
[03:45 - 03:51] the first stage as well as the terminal stage cash flows of $12 billion.
[03:51 - 03:55] You do the same thing using the exit eBITDA multiple approach. Here are the best practices
[03:55 - 04:02] to apply the same multiple that you used in your LBO exit analysis, which we use 7.3.
[04:02 - 04:09] That LBO exit analysis multiple of 7.3 actually implicitly assumes a negative
[04:10 - 04:16] perpetual long-term growth rate, which means that the present value of the enterprise using
[04:16 - 04:23] this multiple approach is $9.8 billion. Question, of course, then, you know, is do you believe
[04:23 - 04:30] that this is a reasonable multiple? And of course, the higher the multiple, the greater the
[04:31 - 04:43] implied perpetual growth rate. So we scroll down here to net debt. We identify that the company
[04:43 - 04:48] has a positive net debt position because of all the new debt that the company's taken on.
[04:49 - 04:58] The WAC calculation, the weighted average cost of capital calculation, now looks at a very highly
[04:58 - 05:04] levered business, 77% of the total capital comes from debt. Now, we've put in these inputs
[05:04 - 05:12] based on the marginal cost of capital for BMC. BMC is most recent borrowing was at 8.125%.
[05:12 - 05:15] Again, those who have gone through our DC, of course, have gone through a whole methodology of
[05:15 - 05:21] what are the different approaches for calculating cost of capital. And then, on the equity side,
[05:21 - 05:24] we go through the same process that we've gone through, which is to look at the risk free rate,
[05:25 - 05:31] add beta times the market risk premium to arrive at a cost of capital here of 7%.
[05:33 - 05:40] Once we've calculated that, we can finally calculate the fair value per share for BMC. Now,
[05:40 - 05:46] the LBO model typically includes a DCF model because it enables us to look at this
[05:46 - 05:51] very table. This is sort of the end result of this DCF model, which says, okay, well, the LBO
[05:51 - 05:57] offer contemplated 6.7 billion dollars in equity for each of these 145 million shares, which means

[06:09 - 06:16] has a share price of 45 dollars and 42 cents, 46 dollars and 25 cents. If you now compare that
[06:16 - 06:21] to the perpetuity approach and the perpetuity approach starts with an enterprise value of 12 billion,
[06:22 - 06:28] given that there's five billion dollars in net debt levered on this company, that brings you
[06:28 - 06:34] to an equity value of 7.3 billion, which amounts to 50 dollars and 58 cents. So that represents a 10%

[06:54 - 06:58] major limitations in that the multiple itself is driven off of a market based approach.

[07:26 - 07:31] or is actually the offer value is higher than what the fair value would be using this approach

[07:44 - 07:49] lower long term growth rate and more negative long term growth rate than a 0% long term growth

### 11. LBO-Modeling-Covenants

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Covenants/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Covenants.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-Covenants\transcript\LBO-Modeling-Covenants.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:09] So we've alluded to covenants earlier, and we talked about how leverage loans have relatively speaking more restrictive covenants than bonds.
[00:09 - 00:16] It's part of a loan, and that's true, as part of a loan, lenders will impose restrictions on borrowers. These restrictions are called covenants.
[00:16 - 00:28] Now financial covenants are the ones you've probably most likely familiar with, and those are basically when the borrower, and that's when the lender says, look, you're the borrower, you want our money, that's great, but you have to be in compliance with certain key ratios.
[00:28 - 00:34] Some of the most common ratios that borrowers have to be in compliance with are debt to be done.
[00:34 - 00:38] So for example, if you want money from us, that's great, but you can never have debt to be done greater than six times.
[00:38 - 00:43] So you can't go out there and borrow more money, or if you're e-pedon deteriorate, it's one or the other. It doesn't matter.
[00:43 - 00:47] You have to be above, I should say you have to be below six times.
[00:47 - 00:57] Why lenders are worried that this company is too levered is going to go bankrupt, so they put in these restrictions to avoid that, to make sure that their loan is safe.
[00:57 - 01:03] e-pedon over interest, interest coverage, related types of ratios, they're the most common e-bid over interest.
[01:03 - 01:07] e-pedon over interest can't be, for example, greater than three times.
[01:07 - 01:13] There are other covenants, like spending limits beyond a pre-specified carve-out with lenders called baskets.
[01:13 - 01:26] So you can't go and buy, spend more on capital expenditures beyond this amount without approval, or just in general, then you have other things like the borrower must pledge to include the lender
[01:26 - 01:33] and any subsequent grant of a security interest. So if you're going to provide other lenders collateral by definition that erodes my collateral.
[01:33 - 01:37] So I want you to pledge that you're going to include me in any subsequent grant.
[01:37 - 01:46] It's called a negative pledge. And if you're downgraded by Moody's or S&P, I want you to automatically pre-pay the loan.
[01:46 - 01:49] It's called a force call.
[01:49 - 01:58] Now very important in the LBL world is, you know, understanding that there's a distinction between what are called maintenance covenants and incurrence covenants.
[01:58 - 02:10] So maintenance covenants are considered the strictest types of covenants. If you borrow a leverage loan, a term loan and revolver that has maintenance covenants, that's considered fairly strict.
[02:10 - 02:12] It's actually fairly a comment at this point as we'll see in a second.
[02:12 - 02:16] It basically requires compliance with covenants every quarter, no matter what.
[02:16 - 02:22] So in other words, you have to maintain compliance with these ratios every quarter, no matter what.
[02:22 - 02:25] Every quarter, you got to send us a report showing us that you're in compliance.
[02:25 - 02:28] That's pretty strict. That's called a maintenance covenants.
[02:28 - 02:33] Well, the more common thing in kind of the below investment grade world isn't called incurrence covenants.
[02:33 - 02:37] Where the lender say, you know what, you know, we're not going to be this strict about this.
[02:37 - 02:43] We require compliance with covenants only when you take a specified action.
[02:43 - 02:46] So for example, if you're going to issue new debt, right?
[02:46 - 02:53] If you're going to borrow more, well, you can't get to worse than you have to be in compliance with this or this, etc.
[02:53 - 03:06] If you borrow more, if you issue dividends, if you make an acquisition, you can understand hopefully that this is a much looser covenant structure than the one where like every quarter, no matter what, you got to report that you're in compliance.
[03:06 - 03:13] So senior debt traditionally includes restrictive maintenance covenants, whereas bonds only include incurrence covenants.
[03:13 - 03:21] I will say that that is in an ideal scenario true and certainly in some sort of, in some credit environments, that's true.
[03:21 - 03:25] Certainly the credit environment we're in right now, that is not, that is less true.
[03:25 - 03:33] The senior debt is more likely than not going to have now incurrence covenants or what's called sort of covenant light.
[03:33 - 03:40] And here's a graphic sort of shows what's happening just in the last several years where increasingly even the leverage loans, right?
[03:40 - 03:47] The most restrictive sort of part of the capital structure are increasingly covenant light.
[03:47 - 03:52] Covenant light really means incurrence covenants, right?
[03:52 - 03:56] So this is called light, okay? This is not.
[03:56 - 04:04] And so that includes only incurrence covenants, which now really amounts to buy, again, this isn't the latest information.
[04:04 - 04:07] Unfortunately, this data just doesn't update as much as we'd like.
[04:07 - 04:13] And that amounts to 75% of new loan issuances by 2018 are covenant light.
[04:13 - 04:25] Okay, so that tells you that as, and it makes sense, it's consistent with all the things we talked about about the current sort of borrowing environment and how easy it is for companies to lever up right now.

### 21. LBO-Modeling-Dry-Powder

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-Dry-Powder\transcript\LBO-Modeling-Dry-Powder.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] Dry powder refers to the available capital that's out there with
[00:03 - 00:08] owned by private equity firms that can be used to do deals.
[00:08 - 00:13] So P firms and other investors have a lot of cash currently waiting to be deployed.
[00:13 - 00:17] And you can see if you look at sort of just traditional buyout that's the red here.
[00:17 - 00:22] You see that the dry powder has grown significantly over the last 10 years.
[00:22 - 00:27] It's ebbden flowed a little bit, but there's been a lot of capital waiting to be deployed.
[00:27 - 00:29] Financial sponsors trying to find deals that are worthwhile.
[00:29 - 00:38] But a consistent problem over the last five years has been that there's a lot of dry powder chasing fewer and fewer good deals.
[00:38 - 00:45] That pressure to put capital to work contributes to higher valuations and multiples when you have a lot of investors that have committed capital.
[00:45 - 00:46] They want to do deals with you.
[00:46 - 00:47] That capital is committed.
[00:47 - 00:53] There's pressure on you as a private equity firm to actually come up with investments as opposed to just doing nothing with that cash.
[00:53 - 00:58] Of course, they do have a responsibility to not do anything if they think that they can't get good deals on.
[00:58 - 01:04] That's the tension that exists between sort of keeping the dry powder, keeping from deploying it and actually deploying it.
[01:04 - 01:09] So there's been a little bit of relief in the last several years in that there have been more exit opportunities.
[01:09 - 01:12] Because the markets are so good, there are a lot of buyers.
[01:12 - 01:18] Private equity funds have been able to sell off portfolio companies that have been sitting there unsellable since the financial crisis.
[01:18 - 01:23] Private equity funds the 2008 vintage, the fund that was raised in 2008.
[01:23 - 01:28] This is the available capital from that fund that hasn't really been deployed yet.
[01:28 - 01:35] You see that if you kind of look at both capital, either past the investment period and you haven't used those funds.
[01:35 - 01:36] This is the amount of funds that haven't been used.
[01:36 - 01:43] If you tack on capital under pressure nearing the end of the investment period before you have to give that capital back, there's a little bit less inaccurate.
[01:43 - 01:44] It's still pretty significant.
[01:44 - 01:53] But if you look at 2011, there's about $200 billion in dry powder from funds that are under pressure that are almost at the expiration of their investment period.
[01:53 - 01:57] Whereas there's about half of that now, a little over half of that now.
[01:57 - 02:05] So the dry powder that's there, even though there's a lot of dry powder more in 2013 than in 2011, it's the nature of that dry powder.
[02:05 - 02:10] It's from newer vintages that puts a little bit less pressure on financial sponsors to put that capital to work.
[02:10 - 02:14] So it's not just an absolute dollar volume of dry powder that's important.
[02:14 - 02:20] It's also the nature of which funds are expiring soon and the dry powder associated with funds.
[02:20 - 02:25] And that takes us really to the next section, which is how do financial sponsors raise capital?
[02:25 - 02:37] And what's sort of the dynamic of the relationship between the investors that provide the capital two financial sponsors called the LPs or limited partners and the private equity firms themselves called the general partners.

### 37. LBO-Modeling-GP-vs-LP-Dynamics

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-GP-vs-LP-Dynamics/transcript/LBO-Modeling-GP-vs-LP-Dynamics.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-GP-vs-LP-Dynamics\transcript\LBO-Modeling-GP-vs-LP-Dynamics.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] So let's talk about some dynamic, some interplay between the general partners and the limited partners.
[00:05 - 00:12] The goal is to have perfect alignment of interest, but the fact of the matter is general partners and LPs are different constituents.
[00:12 - 00:21] They sometimes have competing interests and the way that their relationship is structured is very important to making sure that that relationship is effective.
[00:21 - 00:25] So understand that the limited partners generally have no approval rights over investments.
[00:25 - 00:31] So the pension fund that gives the GP the money doesn't get to approve a deal or not, okay?
[00:31 - 00:38] The capital committed by LPs is, as I've already mentioned, is not drawn immediately, but rather as needed, it is legally binding.
[00:38 - 00:44] When general partners make an investment, they're going to make a capital call drawing down from LPs on a pro-rata basis.
[00:44 - 00:48] When the GPs need more equity capital for a deal.
[00:48 - 00:52] So this is something that doesn't always happen, but certainly.
[00:52 - 00:59] But imagine that you need more equity capital for a deal than you have available as part of the fund.
[00:59 - 01:09] You do see situations where general partners will contact LPs and they'll say, hey, you can co-invest without being charged any carried interest.
[01:09 - 01:13] You can make additional contributions as really true partners of ours.
[01:13 - 01:16] And this is a way to add equity into a deal when needed.
[01:16 - 01:20] You bring in LPs as co-investors.
[01:21 - 01:25] GPs also commit their own capital, but usually just one to five percent of the fund.
[01:25 - 01:28] Again, it really depends on the situation.
[01:28 - 01:32] It could be much more than that, but broadly speaking, one to five percent.
[01:32 - 01:38] Determining the split in distributions between the GPs and the LPs in a private equity fund
[01:38 - 01:40] can actually get pretty complicated.
[01:40 - 01:43] And although it's not directly related to the crux of what we're going to be doing,
[01:43 - 01:48] which is to analyze specific LBO deals, there are some basic parameters
[01:48 - 01:51] that anyone involved in LBOs should understand.
[01:51 - 02:00] And specifically, the sequence of priorities between GPs and LPs in a fund
[02:00 - 02:02] are called the distribution waterfall.
[02:03 - 02:09] And so the general parameters of distribution waterfalls are negotiated between the GPs and the
[02:09 - 02:10] LPs at the onset of the fund.
[02:10 - 02:18] It's an important part of the way the splits are done and are disclosed to potential investors.
[02:18 - 02:24] But they all take most private equity funds, I should say, generally have the same kind of
[02:24 - 02:26] framework around the waterfall.
[02:26 - 02:29] And it starts with a return of investor capital.
[02:29 - 02:34] So anytime the fund sells an investment after they've originally purchased it,
[02:34 - 02:38] now they've exited the investment, presumably through a sale or an IPO or whatever,
[02:38 - 02:44] that the proceeds from that sale, the first thing you do, the first priority is to return
[02:44 - 02:50] the LP or investor capital before any other distributions float anybody else.
[02:51 - 02:53] So that's called return of capital.
[02:53 - 02:58] And it should be noted that any annual management fees, that gets factored into what's considered
[02:58 - 03:00] the return of investor capital.
[03:00 - 03:06] So return of investor capital kind of the original contributions, plus the annual management fees,
[03:06 - 03:10] all the distributions, enough distributions to cover that get first priority.
[03:10 - 03:16] Then investors also usually receive what's called a preferred return or preferred cumulative
[03:16 - 03:18] return, also called a hurdle rate.
[03:18 - 03:24] In the amount of anywhere from 7 to 10 percent annually, usually an average 8 percent annually,
[03:24 - 03:26] prior to the manager getting anything to manage or the GP.
[03:27 - 03:31] So if there are any distributions left over, and of course you'd hope that there are,
[03:31 - 03:34] you're hoping for more than an 8 percent annual return here,
[03:35 - 03:40] to the extent that there are distributions left over after number one and number two,
[03:40 - 03:44] got their returns and keep in mind that managers haven't gotten anything yet at this point.
[03:45 - 03:48] Now the manager usually gets something called a catch up.
[03:48 - 03:50] And the next priority is to the manager.
[03:50 - 03:53] And this catch up is a little tricky to calculate.
[03:53 - 03:58] It basically represents distributions up until the manager has received 20 percent
[03:59 - 04:03] of the amounts distributed under the preferred return tranche,
[04:03 - 04:07] the one right above it here, as well as this current tranche.
[04:07 - 04:10] And so we provided in the footnote a little example of how that would work.
[04:10 - 04:16] If there are $200 million in profits left after a $100 million preferred return distribution,
[04:16 - 04:20] the managers will get priority on $25 million and we're going to calculate that by recognizing
[04:20 - 04:23] that that $100 million preferred return distribution.
[04:24 - 04:30] If we were to sort of true that up divided by 80 percent, we get to $125 million.
[04:30 - 04:36] That's the amount of total distribution that belongs to both managers and investors after
[04:36 - 04:38] they've gotten their capital return.
[04:38 - 04:40] We'll have that $125 million.
[04:40 - 04:44] 100 of course was just returned as a preferred return to investors.
[04:44 - 04:46] There were many $25 million.
[04:46 - 04:49] That's what managers now have priority to.
[04:49 - 04:49] Priority to.
[04:49 - 04:52] So to the extent that there's any distributions after tranche too,
[04:53 - 04:58] these guys, the managers or the GPs get $25 million in priority.
[04:58 - 05:01] Now to the extent that there's anything left over and our simple example here there is,
[05:01 - 05:04] there's about $175 million left over.
[05:04 - 05:09] That's going to be split 80, 20 using the, you know, assuming the carry is 20 percent,
[05:09 - 05:11] usually carry is anywhere from 15 to 20 percent.
[05:11 - 05:15] Assuming 80, 20 carry, that $175 million is going to be carved up 80, 20.

### 42. LBO-Modeling-Interest-Income-and-Circularity

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Interest-Income-and-Circularity/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Interest-Income-and-Circularity.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] So one last thing that I want to do here, and then I'll let you build this schedule yourself, is calculating interest income, right?
[00:06 - 00:13] So historically, we know that the company generated some interest income on its cash balances.
[00:13 - 00:23] And we know that because the company reports interest income on its income statement, 10.6 million and 2012, 8.3 and 2013.
[00:23 - 00:26] We can actually back into the implied interest rate.
[00:26 - 00:32] And this is not a very scientific thing.
[00:32 - 00:37] The interest income presumably is being generated off of average period balances.
[00:37 - 00:39] We just took a short consent.
[00:39 - 00:41] Okay, what's the rough ballpark for the interest rate?
[00:41 - 00:43] It looks like it's about half a percent.
[00:43 - 00:47] And we're going to estimate that to persist going forward.
[00:47 - 00:53] So whatever the cash is going to be, we expect it's going to be 52% or 0.52% going forward.
[00:53 - 00:56] Of course, you could have used an average, nothing wrong with that either.
[00:56 - 01:01] And that enables us to get back into the interest income.
[01:01 - 01:10] So I want to calculate interest income this way.
[01:10 - 01:17] This creates a bit of a circularity because as soon as I plug this back into my model,
[01:17 - 01:25] as soon as I plug that back into the income statement now that I've calculated my interest income,
[01:25 - 01:28] notice what it's going to do to the net income line.
[01:28 - 01:30] It's going to make it higher.
[01:30 - 01:34] But by virtue of making it higher, as soon as I plug that A.2, it made this net income higher,
[01:34 - 01:37] but what does it do to my cash balances?
[01:37 - 01:38] It makes those higher.
[01:38 - 01:39] I just ended it.
[01:39 - 01:40] You see it was lower before?
[01:40 - 01:42] Now it becomes higher.
[01:42 - 01:48] And so what that means is the higher cash balance also makes my interest income from that period
[01:48 - 01:51] in turn higher as well, creating a circularity.
[01:51 - 01:54] In fact, I now have a circular reference in my model.
[01:54 - 01:58] And the way to address that is either by saying something like, you know what?
[01:58 - 02:03] Let me make the calculation for interest income a function of the prior
[02:03 - 02:08] the years end of period balance, thereby avoiding the circularity.
[02:08 - 02:14] And that avoids the circularity completely because now this interest income that is baked in here certainly
[02:14 - 02:18] does lower my raises my net income and raises this cash balance,
[02:18 - 02:22] but in no way affects the interest income that was calculated in this very period.
[02:22 - 02:23] So you avoid the circularity.
[02:23 - 02:28] You see a lot of models avoiding circularities in this way.
[02:28 - 02:31] We don't necessarily want to do that and you see very much why.
[02:31 - 02:36] You can actually significantly get the figure wrong if cash is changing dramatically
[02:36 - 02:37] from one period to the next.
[02:37 - 02:40] So if I think it's much more likely that throughout the period,
[02:40 - 02:43] you're going to have this much cash as opposed to this much cash
[02:43 - 02:46] or you're going to have much less in actual interest income.
[02:46 - 02:49] So how do you deal with that circularity?
[02:49 - 02:52] You have to create a nymph statement.
[02:52 - 02:56] If you want to have the circularity in there, you have to create a nymph statement that says,
[02:56 - 03:04] hey, if the circuit breaker is off, in other words, if there isn't the problem in the model,
[03:04 - 03:06] then leave the formula as is.
[03:06 - 03:09] But if the user turns out circuit breaker on,
[03:09 - 03:14] presumably you would turn this on if the model has blown up because of the circularity.
[03:14 - 03:17] Models don't like circularities because it makes them a little unstable.
[03:17 - 03:20] And sometimes you start seeing the models have div zeros and rough errors everywhere.
[03:20 - 03:23] Of course, we talked about this a lot in the financial statement modeling course.
[03:23 - 03:27] I won't spend too much time on it other than to kind of remind you that this is how you build a circuit breaker.
[03:27 - 03:30] It says, hey, if there's something other than often here,
[03:30 - 03:35] aka the user's turned this to on, you want to flush out the source of the circularity.
[03:35 - 03:39] In this case, that's this cell right here with a zero.
[03:39 - 03:41] So your if false condition becomes zero.
[03:41 - 03:46] Let's anchor this cell, copy and paste this across.
[03:46 - 03:51] And now if the model has blown up, and I can sort of show you what that looks like when you blow up your model.
[03:51 - 03:56] If the model by accident blows up, I can intentionally blow up my model by doing something like this.
[03:56 - 04:01] You see the formula now I just divided this formula by zero and then undid that.
[04:01 - 04:06] So the way to fix it without really ever worrying about it is to bake in the circuit breaker.
[04:06 - 04:08] Let's just do this.
[04:08 - 04:11] And now it's on if flush is zeros out the circularity is flushed out,
[04:11 - 04:14] and then you turn it back off and you're good to go.
[04:14 - 04:18] The one final thing that I'll mention about this is that there's a bit of a shortcut here,
[04:18 - 04:23] in that we're not using average cache balances, but we're using end of period cache balances.
[04:23 - 04:27] So it's actually better to make the assumption that, you know, that I multiply the 0.52%,
[04:27 - 04:36] this cell F-141 by the average of these two.
[04:36 - 04:43] And this is sort of what I alluded to in terms of the historicals that we weren't really doing that.
[04:43 - 04:45] So that makes the interest income a little bit higher.
[04:45 - 04:50] It still has that circularity in it because it's still looking at the end of period balances part of the calculation.
[04:50 - 04:59] And if I wanted to be consistent, I'd have to sort of go back here and calculate the interest rate as a average.
[04:59 - 05:01] You can kind of back into it this way.
[05:01 - 05:06] You do this divided by the average of these two.
[05:06 - 05:09] And that gets you probably more, a more accurate figure.
[05:09 - 05:15] So once you guys go ahead and give that a shot, why don't we use the average beginning of period and end of period balances?
[05:15 - 05:17] And make sure that you integrate this circuit breaker.
[05:17 - 05:20] Just bear in mind that these numbers are all actually going to change.
[05:20 - 05:23] It's just that we're laying down the modeling formulas.
[05:23 - 05:26] The numbers here when all of a sudden, none are going to be a little bit different.

### 43. LBO-Modeling-Introduction-to-LBOs

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Introduction-to-LBOs/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Introduction-to-LBOs.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-Introduction-to-LBOs\transcript\LBO-Modeling-Introduction-to-LBOs.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] An LBO is really just an acquisition where a significant part of the purchase price is funded
[00:05 - 00:10] with debt, whereas the remaining portion is funded with equity that's provided by the financial
[00:10 - 00:11] sponsors.
[00:11 - 00:17] And we're going to use the term financial sponsors, private equity investors, LBO shop interchangeably.
[00:17 - 00:23] They're all basically the investors who provide the equity into an LBO.
[00:23 - 00:28] Now the company that is bought out via a leverage buyout undergoes what is essentially
[00:28 - 00:33] re-capitalization to a now highly leveraged financial structure.
[00:33 - 00:40] We're going to refer to the pre-LBO company as the old co and the new post-LBO company
[00:40 - 00:41] as the new co.
[00:41 - 00:46] Now keep in mind that the companies that are acquired by financial sponsors or PE firms
[00:46 - 00:48] can both be private or public.
[00:48 - 00:53] In fact, the majority of companies that are bought out are private companies, but certainly
[00:53 - 00:57] the public, the GO private, as they're called, the public companies that become private
[00:57 - 01:02] via an LBO are the most high profile ones just because they tend to be bigger and
[01:02 - 01:05] they tend to be more news around them.
[01:05 - 01:11] So the basic intuition that underlies an LBO is actually quite similar to the idea of buying
[01:11 - 01:13] house and financing it with a mortgage.
[01:13 - 01:17] If you've done that or your parents have done that or you know someone who's bought a house
[01:17 - 01:20] with a mortgage, they've basically done an LBO.
[01:20 - 01:24] So let's just go through a very simple LBO which is the process of buying a house.
[01:24 - 01:29] Let's say you buy a house for $500,000 which you finance with $100,000 of your own money.
[01:29 - 01:34] You call the down payment and they're remaining $400,000 you borrow from the bank.
[01:34 - 01:39] Let's say you sell the house five years later and if we assume that you've paid down $150,000
[01:39 - 01:46] of that original $400,000 mortgage and the price of the house has increased by 30%, what
[01:46 - 01:49] does the pie look like now?
[01:49 - 01:54] Well now you're selling a house that's not worth $500,000, but it's gone up in value.
[01:54 - 02:02] it's now $650,000 and now the portion of the value that belongs to the lenders has gone down
[02:02 - 02:07] from $400,000 to $250,000 which means that were you to sell this house you would be able
[02:07 - 02:10] to put $400,000 in your pocket.
[02:10 - 02:15] You started with $100,000 you put $400,000 in your pocket.
[02:15 - 02:21] That's an IRR of 32% and internal rate of return annually, 32% and that's a cash-on-cash
[02:21 - 02:23] return of four times.
[02:23 - 02:27] You put $100,000 and you got back $400,000.
[02:27 - 02:35] That's the basic contour of an LBL, the idea, if you whittle it down, the basic idea behind
[02:35 - 02:39] the LBL is find a business, lever it up.
[02:39 - 02:44] In other words, put in very little money of your own, borrow as much money as you can from
[02:44 - 02:50] the bank at as good a set of terms as possible, try to grow the business operationally
[02:50 - 02:56] so that it increases in value and try to pay down as much debt as possible so that when
[02:56 - 03:02] all is said and done your value, the value that you own in the business becomes greater
[03:02 - 03:05] and greater and greater.
[03:05 - 03:11] So let's talk about the way that this is actually structured.
[03:11 - 03:16] So financial sponsors finance deals with a lot of debt and put up a relatively small amount
[03:16 - 03:22] of equity as debt is paid down and the value of the business grows sponsors earn large
[03:22 - 03:23] returns.
[03:23 - 03:29] So the key drivers of success are, I think, fairly straightforward.
[03:29 - 03:30] You want to get in cheap.
[03:30 - 03:33] In other words, you want to buy that business as cheaply as possible.
[03:33 - 03:37] Now that's true regardless of what type of investor you are, you always want to get
[03:37 - 03:38] in cheap.
[03:38 - 03:44] But with LBLs, it's actually very important to be able to find assets that are deemed
[03:44 - 03:49] to be undervalued in the market because that's going to accelerate the ability to pay down
[03:49 - 03:51] that debt and unlock equity.
[03:51 - 03:53] Next driver of success is levering with a lot of cheap debt.
[03:53 - 03:57] Obviously without that leverage, you don't generate the kinds of returns that you would
[03:57 - 04:01] have otherwise and we can go back to the house example and actually just take a look.
[04:01 - 04:06] If you don't leverage this business up, it goes, let's say you funded it all with equity.
[04:06 - 04:13] It'd be a $500,000 house funded with a $500,000 worth of equity so it goes up by 30%.
[04:13 - 04:21] If you sell it for $650, sure, you generated $150,000 in profit on your original investment
[04:21 - 04:22] of $500,000.
[04:22 - 04:27] But that is, by no means, a 32% IR, that's not a four-time, four X.
[04:27 - 04:31] That's a 1.2 X or whatever that number comes out to.
[04:31 - 04:36] So leverage is what jacks up your returns pretty significantly.
[04:36 - 04:38] So that's kind of getting back here.
[04:38 - 04:42] So getting in cheap, levering with a lot of cheap debt and improving the business, obviously,
[04:42 - 04:47] through the extent that you can grow the business, that's going to enable you to very
[04:47 - 04:51] quickly recoup money and generate significant returns.
[04:51 - 04:56] Last but not least, being able to exit within three to five years at a high multiple, a high
[04:56 - 05:00] multiple means that either you've grown the business and the structural sort of profile
[05:00 - 05:05] of the business is such that people are willing to pay more per e-dollar of e-bada than
[05:05 - 05:08] they would have, you know, when you bought the company, so if you bought the company seven
[05:08 - 05:11] times e-bada, you sell it nine times e-bada.
[05:11 - 05:14] The way that that happens is one, if you really structurally improve the business, and
[05:14 - 05:18] secondly, if you're lucky and you bought in a sort of macroeconomic environment where
[05:18 - 05:23] you're industry and your company is being valued low, but then you sell in good financial
[05:23 - 05:24] times.
[05:24 - 05:26] So that's a little bit of luck as well.
[05:26 - 05:29] So that's ultimately the goal.
[05:29 - 05:34] If you're successful, if you sort of achieve all four of these basic drivers.
[05:34 - 05:37] Now, there's a lot more to each one of those and we're going to spend some time really
[05:37 - 05:41] thinking about each one of those as we build through our models, but let's just talk a
[05:41 - 05:46] little bit more in depth about how those profits are actually generated.
[05:46 - 05:50] So specifically, when we're talking about exiting, what do we mean by exiting?
[05:50 - 05:58] Well, the most common way that private equity firms exit is they sell to a strategic
[05:58 - 06:00] acquire or another PE firm.
[06:00 - 06:06] In fact, you know, close to you can tell over the last several years, close to 90 or
[06:06 - 06:12] or even over 90% of all exits of all private equity exits are either selling to a strategic,
[06:12 - 06:17] which they call here a corporate acquisition or selling to another PE firm, which they
[06:17 - 06:19] call a secondary buyout.
[06:19 - 06:26] The next option is to IPO to basically go public to sell shares to the open public.
[06:26 - 06:31] There's actually a third alternative, which is to do what's called a dividend recapitalization.
[06:31 - 06:33] And this is something that's increasing in common.
[06:33 - 06:37] It's also indicative of kind of a more loose credit environment where sponsors can also
[06:37 - 06:43] monetize without a complete exit by simply giving themselves a dividend financed by new borrowing.
[06:43 - 06:47] So imagine, again, going back to this house example where you get to this point and you
[06:47 - 06:51] go back to the same bank that gave you that original 400,000 and you say, hey, you know what?
[06:51 - 06:54] We were able to pay down $150,000 of that debt.
[06:54 - 06:58] Obviously, you are comfortable with this level of leverage back then.
[06:58 - 07:01] Let's leverage this company up again to the same 400,000.
[07:01 - 07:04] So give us another $150,000.
[07:04 - 07:09] So we'll lever it back up to 400,000 and we'll just issue ourselves a dividend.
[07:09 - 07:12] We being the owners of the business, the equity owners of the business will issue ourselves
[07:12 - 07:14] $150,000 dividend.
[07:14 - 07:16] That's called a dividend recap.
[07:16 - 07:21] Very simple process that tends to be a signal that credit markets are pretty loose.
[07:21 - 07:23] There were no dividend recaps during the financial crisis, for example.

[00:00 - 00:07] Finally, we get to the big show in private equity, and that is the debt portion of the capital structure.

[00:32 - 00:36] And this is a question of several factors.
[00:36 - 00:41] The amount of debt that can be raised in a deal depends on a variety of factors.
[00:41 - 00:43] First, the size and the stability of the cash flows.
[00:43 - 00:49] All else equal, the more stable, the more predictable, the more reliable the cash flows in a company.
[00:49 - 00:53] The more willing a lender is going to be to lend money.
[00:53 - 01:05] Generally, and this is sort of a related concept, there is a preference for defensive, less cyclical firms, firms that are going to be less vulnerable to kind of market fluctuations.
[01:05 - 01:08] Again, this has to do with that stability and the reliability of cash flows.

[01:14 - 01:17] principal payments that you're going to have to make.

[01:24 - 01:31] The higher credibility, the financial sponsor, the easier it's going to be for that sponsor to secure financing.
[01:31 - 01:35] And lastly, perhaps most importantly, is the lending environment.
[01:35 - 01:41] For example, if we're part of a cycle where credit is cheap, credit is loose, it's easy to find financing.
[01:41 - 01:47] Well, all of a sudden, you're going to start seeing leverage ratios creep up, debt capacity increase.
[01:47 - 01:50] And that's in fact the environment that we find ourselves in now.

### 47. LBO-Modeling-LBO-Capital-Structure---Equity

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] We are now ready to start talking about the heart of the LBO, and that's its capital structure.
[00:05 - 00:11] So while capital structure, the percentage that comes in the form of equity versus debt has been cyclical,
[00:11 - 00:18] there's also been what we sort of call a structural shift from equity to debt ratios of 20 to 80% in the 80s,
[00:18 - 00:21] to closer to 40 to 60% more recently.
[00:21 - 00:25] You kind of see that in this chart here, where although it doesn't go back as far as the 80s,
[00:25 - 00:33] you do see far lower sort of equity contributions historically in what we've seen as a shift to this high kind of 40,
[00:33 - 00:40] maybe even higher than 40, 45% closer to 45% when in more recent years I should say.
[00:40 - 00:46] Let's talk a little bit about that equity component in more detail.
[00:46 - 00:52] So majority of the equity, as we've been talking about, comes from financial sponsors.
[00:52 - 00:56] They represent the largest source of LBO equity in a deal.
[00:56 - 01:01] But in addition to financial sponsors, there are several other sources of equity capital.
[01:01 - 01:07] One of the biggest sources of equity capital is rollover, typically from management or existing shareholders.
[01:07 - 01:16] In some cases, old-cone management, or again, existing shareholders, will roll over their existing equity from the old co into the new co,
[01:16 - 01:21] and even potentially contribute new capital alongside the sponsors.
[01:21 - 01:26] Management rollover has historically ranged from 2% to 5% of the total equity in an LBO,
[01:26 - 01:31] but you have many LBOs with no management rollover and some LBOs with tons of management rollover.
[01:31 - 01:39] And in fact, Michael Dell rolled over 3.6 billion dollars of equity and contributed an extra 750 million dollars of fresh cash.
[01:39 - 01:45] So to the extent that the private equity from Silver Lake that acquired Dell alongside Michael Dell,
[01:45 - 01:50] they actually contributed less in equity themselves directly than Michael Dell did in total.
[01:50 - 01:58] So the 24 billion dollars came from a whole bunch of debt, and some equity, most of that came from Michael Dell.
[01:58 - 02:01] In addition, he also contributed 750 million dollars of cash.
[02:01 - 02:06] So that's money that had they bought out Michael Dell, Silver Lake would have had to pony up.
[02:06 - 02:10] They didn't have to because he's rolling over that old co equity into the new co.
[02:10 - 02:13] We're going to talk a little bit more in this.
[02:13 - 02:19] We're actually going to go through a simple sort of case study using the Michael, the Dell LBO before we dive into our full case study.
[02:19 - 02:22] So just stay tuned for that in just a moment.
[02:22 - 02:26] Now there are other sources of equity, specifically the option pool.
[02:26 - 02:31] And what I mean, what that refers to is that in addition to rollover or sponsor equity,
[02:31 - 02:36] most LBOs have old co management stay on to run the new co.
[02:36 - 02:47] And regardless of whether they roll over their equity or not, sponsors reserve anywhere from 3 to up to 20% of the total equity for them to receive over
[02:47 - 02:50] time in the form of stock options.
[02:50 - 02:57] So for staying on, if you want to keep people on, if you want to bring in top talent, you're going to have to reserve a portion of the equity.
[02:57 - 03:01] You can't keep all the equity to yourself if the financial sponsors kept 100% of the equity.
[03:01 - 03:03] They didn't reserve anything for an option pool.
[03:03 - 03:08] They wouldn't be able to retain top management and they wouldn't be able to attract new professionals.
[03:08 - 03:15] And so to the extent that you're in an industry where that's very important, you can see option pools of anywhere from 3 to 20% of the total equity,
[03:15 - 03:18] which dilute obviously the sponsor equity.
[03:18 - 03:20] Last but not least, warrants.
[03:20 - 03:26] So certain lenders, and we're going to talk about this shortly, may receive equity as a sweetener for providing financing.
[03:26 - 03:29] Specifically, I'm talking about mezzanine financing.
[03:29 - 03:35] Mezzanine lenders, these are lenders that provide high yield bonds, but maybe want some sort of equity kicker as it's called,
[03:35 - 03:40] or some sort of additional sweetener to juice up returns.
[03:40 - 03:43] Typically, that comes in the form of warrants, and it's sort of on the back end of the deal.
[03:43 - 03:52] It says, okay, when you have to pay back our principal, you also have to give us what amounts to whatever 3% of the total equity.
[03:52 - 03:54] So that dilutes sponsors even further.
[03:54 - 03:57] So that's something that we're going to have to think about when we build our real model.
[03:57 - 04:01] How do you incorporate the potential dilution from mezzanine financing and things of that nature?

[00:00 - 00:03] Now, let's talk about mezzanine financing.
[00:03 - 00:10] So mezzanine financing is sort of broadly refers to financing that sits between debt and equity.
[00:10 - 00:16] We're talking about convertible debt, bond with warrants, or bond issue that has warrants
[00:16 - 00:17] attached to it.
[00:17 - 00:21] A convertible preferred stock, that is preferred stock that can be convertible to common
[00:21 - 00:25] stock, or just straight up preferred stock with warrants attached to it.
[00:25 - 00:32] And then lastly, unsecured debt with few or any covenants.
[00:32 - 00:37] So this is what we're talking about when we refer to mezzanine financing.
[00:37 - 00:42] The pricing components of it is the mezzanine investors are usually looking for some sort
[00:42 - 00:48] of blended return to 15 to 20% and by blended, I mean, the actual return on their high
[00:48 - 00:54] yield bond issue, on the debt that they're lending, on the loan, or the preferred stock.
[00:54 - 00:58] So, you know, the return of the dividend, the dividend return.
[00:58 - 01:03] But in addition to that, they always have an equity kicker component to it.
[01:03 - 01:07] That's sort of the hallmark of a mezzanine loan, that it's not just the loan with a fixed,
[01:07 - 01:12] you know, with a fixed interest rate or a fixed dividend, but there's also the equity
[01:12 - 01:13] component.
[01:13 - 01:16] So together, they're targeting blended returns of 15 to 20%.
[01:16 - 01:22] And those returns are in the form of, again, cash interest, dividends, pick interest,
[01:22 - 01:27] dividends, along with an equity kicker and pick interest, represents payment and kind.
[01:27 - 01:29] It is, it is, it is interest expense.
[01:29 - 01:34] It's not paid out immediately, but simply accrues to the very end to when the principle
[01:34 - 01:37] is due.
[01:37 - 01:41] Hedge funds and mezzanine funds are the primary investors in this type of financing.
[01:41 - 01:44] They're the primary providers of capital and they often tailor the investment to meet
[01:44 - 01:46] the specific needs of the deal.
[01:46 - 01:54] So this is, this can take kind of any shape or size, but what makes this, the common features
[01:54 - 01:59] of mezzanine financing is that it's a sort of straight type of financing, whether it's
[01:59 - 02:06] a high yield debt or a preferred stock with this equity component.
[02:06 - 02:10] I will just caution again a terminology mezzanine is sometimes more loosely defined as financing
[02:10 - 02:15] between secured debt inequity, which would place our plain vanilla high yield bonds into
[02:15 - 02:16] the category.
[02:16 - 02:20] So for consistency purposes and just so that we're always on the same page, we're going
[02:20 - 02:24] to exclusively refer to mezzanine financing throughout this course as financing specifically
[02:24 - 02:29] below plain old high yield bonds where there's some sort of blended return, that includes
[02:29 - 02:33] equity.
[02:33 - 02:37] In addition to the capital structure components that we've talked about up till now,
[02:37 - 02:46] there's a nuance to LBO and M&A financing, which is that there's a time period between
[02:46 - 02:53] when the deal is sort of negotiated and when the deal actually closes.
[02:53 - 02:59] And during that time, usually you see financing called a bridge or interim financing.
[02:59 - 03:05] Bridge loans provide interim financing, should the LBO debt not be available by the closing
[03:05 - 03:06] of the deal?
[03:06 - 03:10] And so for the old co shareholders, it's very important to ensure that this financing
[03:10 - 03:13] that's being discussed is actually available.
[03:13 - 03:16] So bridge loans are usually something that comes from an investment banks in the form
[03:16 - 03:23] of a commitment that says, hey, if the private equity folks aren't successful in securing
[03:23 - 03:28] the loans that they're talking about securing, you guys are going to be okay.
[03:28 - 03:33] You being the old co shareholders because we are going to provide interim financing.
[03:33 - 03:37] So it's a commitment by the investment bank to kind of provide this bridge financing.

[04:27 - 04:32] there been a balance, there could be pick interest, there could also be some cash interest
[04:32 - 04:35] for the subordinated notes, right?
[04:35 - 04:39] So that all plays that's sort of a standard feature of subordinated bonds.
[04:39 - 04:43] And then you have preferred stock which have pick accruals potentially, assuming a certain
[04:43 - 04:46] pick rate, and then a cash rate as well.
[04:46 - 04:51] So those are the typical characteristics, but another common characteristic, especially
[04:51 - 04:57] in an LBL like this, is that they may also get the right to get some sort of equity
[04:57 - 04:58] upside.

[06:02 - 06:08] Where it sort of flows, you know, the cash goes down and retain earnings goes down by the interest expense
[06:08 - 06:12] In this case pick interest you retain earnings goes down by interest expense
[06:12 - 06:14] But cash doesn't go out the door in fact
[06:14 - 06:20] It's just a cruise to the principal of the loan and when the company ends up having to repay the entire principal
[06:20 - 06:24] It also has to repay all this accrued interest component as well. That's called pick interest
[06:24 - 06:27] So that's gonna be a plus we're gonna calculate that in just a little bit
[06:29 - 06:32] For now we just got the place holders
[06:32 - 06:36] We are good to go. We're gonna leave the pick interest off for a moment
[06:38 - 06:40] And preferred stock we're gonna leave that off

[06:44 - 06:46] So now

[02:48 - 02:54] So, any increases is actually we got to reverse that because if you have to borrow more money,
[02:54 - 02:58] that means that the revolving credit lenders have to provide that money.
[02:58 - 03:10] And then, of course, any interest payments to the revolver are inflows to the revolver, right?

[03:29 - 03:35] when the financial sponsor is exit and presumably they're going to sell their company to whoever,
[03:35 - 03:43] we're assuming for the purposes of returns that the exit means that everyone gets out that the debt is going to be refinance by somebody else.
[03:43 - 03:47] So, they're going to also get the principal balance back.

[00:00 - 00:06] So one issue that's emerged post tax reform is the corporate structure of PE firms and their portfolio companies.
[00:06 - 00:17] Specifically, the maybe PE firms are going to think about structuring their portfolio companies no longer as partnerships or pass through entities, but as sea corporations.
[00:17 - 00:20] So here's what the current status quo is.
[00:20 - 00:24] Private equity firms are predominantly structured as limited partnerships.
[00:24 - 00:37] That is a pass through entity where basically you pay one level of tax and you're essentially taxed any sort of tax from the company is just passed through directly in your taxes and individual as a partner in this partnership.
[00:37 - 00:41] PE funds themselves are also structured as LPs.
[00:41 - 00:46] So the PE firms create PE funds which are also structured as LPs as passed through entities.
[00:46 - 00:54] Everything is passing through from the portfolio company directly to individuals as sort of the bottom line as opposed to a sea corporations which get a double tax.
[00:54 - 01:06] PE portfolio companies themselves are also usually structured as passed through entities as LLCs or as S corporations, but there are some sea corporations as well.
[01:06 - 01:16] So think of the portfolio companies as the businesses, but the way that the profits from those businesses pass through to the owners is using passed through structure.
[01:16 - 01:21] So here is a visual representation of what typically happens.
[01:21 - 01:30] You have portfolio companies which are usually structured as passed through, so though there are some sea corporations which then you do get sort of dinged on dividends etc.
[01:30 - 01:39] Most are passed throughs which means any profits generated in those portfolio companies pass through directly to the funds that own them.
[01:39 - 01:50] The funds themselves are companies are limited partnerships I should say that are set up by the financial sponsor, the PE firms and the PE firm sets up a new fund every few years.
[01:50 - 01:53] And the PE fund holds portfolio companies.
[01:53 - 01:55] It's through the fund that those investments are made.
[01:55 - 02:04] And the PE fund sends fees and carried interest which we'll talk about in just a second.
[02:04 - 02:16] Back to the sponsors that's how they are in their living and in exchange the sponsor is doing all the administration running the business, finding portfolio companies to invest in, helping to install operators and them etc.
[02:16 - 02:25] So the sponsors doing the work, the investment, finding the investments, helping to run the investments and then the associated fees and carried interest flow through.
[02:25 - 02:32] These are structured as limited partnerships, this fund is structured as a limited partnership and this is usually also structured as a passed through entity.
[02:32 - 02:44] And then the investors that they used to help fund the PE fund provide cash in exchange the funds kick back interest on the capital that was provided originally.
[02:44 - 02:50] So this is the typical structure and so now let's talk about specific tax rates host tax reform.
[02:50 - 03:01] You have lower tax rates for C corporations, so pre tax reform is 35% and then you had 23.8% shareholder level tax for total cumulative tax of 50.5%.
[03:01 - 03:05] Well current it's 39.8% it's gone down to 21%.
[03:05 - 03:13] And so you've got a significant sort of reduction in cumulative tax, you got two levels of tax it brings it down to 40%.
[03:13 - 03:15] Well with partnerships it's a lot trickier.
[03:15 - 03:28] So partnerships pre tax reform was just a straight 39.6% and so now you have the best case scenario is you've got to go down to 33.4% right.
[03:28 - 03:31] We've got the footnotes explaining why we talked about this last week as well.
[03:31 - 03:43] That's your best case scenario and then assumes that all of your income sort of qualifies in the technical term for this post tax reform is that your qualified business income gets a full 20% deduction.
[03:43 - 03:51] But it doesn't always qualify and if your business income doesn't qualify you've got a you're facing a tax rate of 40.8%.
[03:51 - 04:01] Right so what what companies are facing is structuring is a pass through kind of range the tax rate ranges from 33.4 to 40.8% now compared to 39.6 before.
[04:01 - 04:07] So it's either kind of flat compared to before or lower but then for C corporations it's an absolute good.
[04:07 - 04:17] All right so C corpse continued to face higher relative tax usually right assuming that they get the QBI deduction but the gap is narrowed.
[04:17 - 04:27] So again C corporation tax remains less attractive and absolute terms most of the time but it's more attractive relative to what it was pre TCJ.
[04:27 - 04:33] So the decision to be structured as a C corp versus a pass through is now a tougher decision.
[04:33 - 04:41] And this is especially the case if you don't qualify for the QBI deduction there are several exceptions the deduction is still subject to limits and phase outs.
[04:41 - 04:51] Again this is where you know talking to a qualified tax consultant becomes important because without the QBI deduction C corporation tax rates actually are lower than partnerships.
[04:51 - 04:58] Remember that going back you got a 48% tax if you're structured as a partnership versus a 39.8% as a C corp.
[04:58 - 05:05] So it's really been kind of an alignment between sort of you know structuring both as pass through versus a C corporations.
[05:05 - 05:10] You no longer have this big gap in benefits that it's better to structure as a pass through.

[04:24 - 04:29] that put upward, you know, that actually creates some benefits on returns despite the fact
[04:29 - 04:33] that if you kind of look at one particular element of it, which is the higher leverage,
[04:33 - 04:37] the higher interest or the maximum interest expense deduction, it may seem like, oh, this
[04:37 - 04:38] is not good for private equity.
[04:38 - 04:42] I think if you take it all into totality, it is good for private equity, at least that's
[04:42 - 04:43] what most people believe at this point.

### 75. LBO-Modeling-Tax-savings-in-LBOs

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Tax-savings-in-LBOs/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Tax-savings-in-LBOs.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-Tax-savings-in-LBOs\transcript\LBO-Modeling-Tax-savings-in-LBOs.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:08] Earlier on, we talked about the things that sponsors look for in an LBO, what kind of traits of companies make for good LBOs.
[00:08 - 00:13] And we mentioned in one of those bullets that tax savings can be achieved through leveraging up,
[00:13 - 00:17] through finding an optimal capital structure, in other words, putting on more debt.
[00:17 - 00:22] And you can achieve these tax savings. Let's circle back and talk about that because there have been some changes
[00:22 - 00:26] in sort of the value proposition of changing the capital structure.
[00:26 - 00:29] And I think it's important for us to spend just a couple of minutes making sure we understand
[00:29 - 00:34] the general framework before we get to our modeling exercises where we dive deeper into this stuff.
[00:35 - 00:44] So until recently, interest was fully tax deductible. And that means that the cost of debt
[00:44 - 00:51] is essentially subsidized by the government in the form of lower taxes. So imagine at a 25% tax
[00:51 - 00:58] rate, a company's nominal borrowing cost of 5%, let's say the coupon is 5%, so you're borrowing
[00:58 - 01:04] at 5%. Well, the amount that you actually have to pay, the sort of the effective cost of debt
[01:04 - 01:13] is 5 times 1 minus that tax rate, 1 minus 25% is 75%. So it's 3.75%. And that's because
[01:14 - 01:21] interest expense reduces your tax expense because it's tax deductible. So a dollar of interest expense
[01:21 - 01:28] actually only costs you 75 cents is what that's saying. And so at a 5% interest rate,
[01:29 - 01:35] you're only really looking at the 3.75% interest rate. And so all else equal, this benefit,
[01:35 - 01:41] this tax deduction increases equity IRRs higher than they would have been. Had there been
[01:41 - 01:47] no such tax deduction. Now, one thing to understand the next several minutes that we're going to
[01:47 - 01:54] spend talking about this is understanding that there are three things that are driving how pronounced
[01:54 - 01:59] this tax deduction benefit is. The first is how much leverage you put on, right? All else equal,
[01:59 - 02:03] the more leverage the more interest expense you're going to have. And therefore, the more tax
[02:03 - 02:08] benefits you're going to get from deducting interest expense. The next thing is the tax rate itself.
[02:08 - 02:15] So we just use 25% in our simple example here. Well, the higher the tax rate, the more pronounced
[02:15 - 02:23] the benefit and vice versa. And lastly, the interest rate itself, the higher the cost of capital,
[02:23 - 02:28] the more pronounced the benefit because there's now more dollars that are tax deductible.
[02:30 - 02:37] Now, since the 1980s, all three of these really when LBOs kind of came to the four, all three of
[02:37 - 02:43] these have decreased. These, the, in other words, leverage on LBOs has generally gone down.
[02:43 - 02:49] As we just saw, it's down to 60% now. It's cyclical in the sense that it's gone up from, say,
[02:49 - 02:54] the right, you know, immediately post financial crisis levels, but compared to what it was in the
[02:54 - 03:00] 80s, when leverage was 80%, 90% of the capital structure, it's gone down. Same with the tax rate.
[03:00 - 03:05] We're about to talk about that tax rates specifically after the 20, and especially in the United
[03:05 - 03:11] States after the 2017 tax reform, tax rates have gone down significantly. And lastly, interest rates
[03:11 - 03:15] generally have gone down since the 1980s, right? They're going up, as of the recording in this
[03:15 - 03:20] video, they're going up off of kind of their historical lows, but interest rates are generally
[03:20 - 03:25] lower. All, in fact, all three of these things that are driving how valuable this tax deduction
[03:25 - 03:30] benefit is, all three of these things are less pronounced than they used to be. And so the tax
[03:30 - 03:35] savings associated with levering up is less than it used to be. It doesn't mean that it doesn't
[03:35 - 03:37] exist. It's just less than it used to be.

[03:07 - 03:14] together. So purchases of software development costs as well as purchases of intangible assets
[03:14 - 03:22] get you to this number here. And we get cash from investing activities. So really the cash
[03:22 - 03:27] from investing activities takes about a $200 million chunk out of the billion dollars in operating
[03:27 - 03:34] cash and roughly speaking operating cash net of investing outflows is what we call free cash flows.

### 80. LBO-Modeling-What-makes-a-good-LBO

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-What-makes-a-good-LBO/transcript/LBO-Modeling-What-makes-a-good-LBO.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-What-makes-a-good-LBO\transcript\LBO-Modeling-What-makes-a-good-LBO.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] What do investors look for in finding good LBL?
[00:03 - 00:07] So depending on the industry, depending on the company, there's a lot of factors.
[00:07 - 00:12] But if we're talking in broad strokes, a company with steady cash flows, with little
[00:12 - 00:18] or limited cyclicality, meaning that their cash flows aren't sort of volatile depending
[00:18 - 00:24] on the economic cycle, that's the type of business that generally make for a good LBL.
[00:24 - 00:28] And the reason is when you lever up a business with a ton of debt, you now have massive
[00:28 - 00:33] fixed interest in principal payments every year that are really reducing your wiggle
[00:33 - 00:40] room in terms of how much a decline in revenues can the business can withstand.
[00:40 - 00:47] So steady cash flows that are predictable, that can be levered up with large fixed interest
[00:47 - 00:53] in principal payments, company that isn't very volatile because of cyclicality issues,
[00:53 - 00:55] that's a good candidate.
[00:55 - 01:00] A company with minimal ongoing capital expenditures and working capital needs all else equal
[01:00 - 01:05] is better because ongoing capital expenditures and working capital needs drain investments
[01:05 - 01:08] from the business, they could otherwise cash flows from the business, they could otherwise
[01:08 - 01:12] go towards debt service.
[01:12 - 01:18] Suboptimal capital structure is something that is really attractive from a financial
[01:18 - 01:24] sponsor standpoint because if a company has low leverage, they're not generating
[01:24 - 01:30] the tax savings that the tax shield associated with interest expense provides.
[01:30 - 01:34] And so you can actually just create value simply by relivering the business without doing
[01:34 - 01:37] anything else.
[01:37 - 01:45] Because you can deduct interest expense payments but not dividends from your earnings, it
[01:45 - 01:48] makes sense to lever up a business to a certain point.
[01:48 - 01:54] And if a business has no leverage, then you can actually create value just by increasing
[01:54 - 01:56] the leverage in a business.
[01:56 - 02:00] Next, if a business is deemed undervalued in the market, in other words, a trading at
[02:00 - 02:06] a low multiple or it's an industry that's fallen out of favor but has something left,
[02:06 - 02:10] might be turned around, those are the kinds of opportunities that LBO financial sponsors
[02:10 - 02:16] tend to look at because it means they can buy cheap and they can turn things around,
[02:16 - 02:22] assuming they can turn things around, they can really extract significant returns.
[02:22 - 02:27] Next, and this is fairly common in LBO and maybe a little counterintuitively but management
[02:27 - 02:32] teams tend to stick around post-LBO or at least portions of them.
[02:32 - 02:36] And what financial sponsors often look for are strong management teams that maybe aren't
[02:36 - 02:40] operating optimally as a public company.
[02:40 - 02:45] So if we're talking specifically in this case about public companies, LBO sponsors would
[02:45 - 02:49] look at, financial sponsors would look at a public company and say, you know what?
[02:49 - 02:55] There's perhaps a lot of investor pressure put on this company to meet quarterly, for
[02:55 - 02:59] example, earnings milestones that don't necessarily do the company well, whereas perhaps
[02:59 - 03:05] the company might need to restructure, miss several quarters of earnings estimates, but
[03:05 - 03:08] that's the right thing to do from a long-term and when I say long-term, I mean three to five
[03:08 - 03:14] years, sort of value creation, well that's the type of company that might have management
[03:14 - 03:18] that wants to do that but feels like it'll get fired by the public shareholders if it
[03:18 - 03:24] does that, that might work with a private equity firm to go private and to operate that
[03:24 - 03:30] way under the not being under the microscope of the public investors.
[03:30 - 03:35] Last but not least, and this is certainly not a fundamental part of a good LBO but it can
[03:35 - 03:39] be an important part of an LBO and something that makes an LBO very attractive and that
[03:39 - 03:44] is if there are subsidiaries that can be sold to pay down the debt pretty instantly.
[03:44 - 03:48] So if part of the strategy in an LBO is to look at the value of a business from the
[03:48 - 03:52] some of the parts type of approach and say, you know what, this business, if you really
[03:52 - 03:56] broke it up and looked at all the individual parts, it's actually very undervalued compared
[03:56 - 04:01] to what it would look like if you just broke it up, sold off certain pieces and sort
[04:01 - 04:07] of right sized it to what it would eat into something a little bit more viable as a going
[04:07 - 04:11] concern, that's a strategy that's actually not uncommon in LBOs as well.
[04:11 - 04:16] So subsidiaries clearly definable that can be sold to pay down debt instantly so that the smaller
[04:16 - 04:21] now going concern is more nimble, that's something that happens quite a bit as well.

### 82. LBO-Modeling-Whole-Fund-vs-Deal-by-deal-Distribution-Model

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Whole-Fund-vs-Deal-by-deal-Distribution-Model/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Whole-Fund-vs-Deal-by-deal-Distribution-Model.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_LBO\LBO-Modeling-Whole-Fund-vs-Deal-by-deal-Distribution-Model\transcript\LBO-Modeling-Whole-Fund-vs-Deal-by-deal-Distribution-Model.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] As investments are sold by the fund, there are actually two ways you can
[00:04 - 00:09] calculate distributions. One is the whole fund model, in which case manager doesn't get
[00:09 - 00:14] any carry until investors get full capital and preferred return payments.
[00:14 - 00:17] That's kind of what we've been talking about in the prior slide.
[00:17 - 00:23] But the deal-by-deal model looks at distributions calculated on a deal-by-deal basis.
[00:23 - 00:28] Whatever the LP contributes in terms of capital for a specific investment,
[00:28 - 00:31] that's what's returned first in that waterfall,
[00:31 - 00:34] not the aggregate capital contribution to the fund.
[00:34 - 00:38] And that changes the timing of cash flows that are coming in.
[00:38 - 00:42] Because it means that the GP's could be getting
[00:42 - 00:48] carry before long before they would on a whole fund model.
[00:48 - 00:52] And that creates a little bit of an issue which private equity funds
[00:52 - 00:56] address and LP is addressed by incorporating clawback provisions and deals.
[00:56 - 01:00] So generally, deal-by-deal accelerates payments to managers.
[01:00 - 01:04] And of course, that should make sense if you do a good deal and you sell that
[01:04 - 01:08] investment and it generates a profit above the original basis.
[01:08 - 01:12] You're going to calculate carry on that particular deal because you're not
[01:12 - 01:15] doing on a whole fund basis, you're doing on a deal-by-deal basis.
[01:15 - 01:18] But one thing to keep in mind is because of something called the clawback
[01:18 - 01:23] provision, it doesn't actually increase the amount the managers
[01:23 - 01:26] ultimately receive. And that's because clawback provisions ensure that
[01:26 - 01:31] investors will reclaim carry at the end when the aggregate distributions
[01:31 - 01:34] are calculated and are determined to be potentially insufficient
[01:34 - 01:37] to fully provide capital and preferred returns.
[01:37 - 01:44] Then the LP's will clawback the any carry and that's going to apply.
[01:44 - 01:48] And that's going to make both these models equal in terms of how much money
[01:48 - 01:49] at the end of the day everyone keeps.
[01:49 - 01:53] The difference is of course that if there are no clawbacks,
[01:53 - 02:00] if the fund is successful and everybody gets their return,
[02:00 - 02:01] their capital and preferred returns.
[02:01 - 02:04] And then some, well then in that case, the difference is
[02:04 - 02:08] that on a deal-by-deal model, the private equity
[02:08 - 02:12] GPs are going to get their carry earlier than they would have
[02:12 - 02:16] on a whole fund model. So that's a primary difference. And again,
[02:16 - 02:20] whether or not the structure is going to be whole fund or deal-by-deal or some sort of
[02:20 - 02:25] something in between, there are also complicated structures that try to
[02:25 - 02:27] kind of mix and match these models.
[02:27 - 02:30] That's going to be a function of the original structure
[02:30 - 02:36] and negotiation between the GPs and the LP's of the overall fund.

---

### 84. LBO-Modeling-Working-Through-the-Dell-LBO-on-a-Cocktail-Napkin-Part-2

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_LBO/LBO-Modeling-Working-Through-the-Dell-LBO-on-a-Cocktail-Napkin-Part-2/transcript/LBO-Modeling-Working-Through-the-Dell-LBO-on-a-Cocktail-Napkin-Part-2.transcript.txt`
